ترجمه: (۵) صرف جسم سے الگ ہونے سے پانی مستعمل ہو جاتا ہے۔ تشریح : پانی کب مستعمل ہوگا اسکے تین مقامات ہیں (۱) عضو پر رہتے ہوئے ہی پانی مستعمل شمار کیا جائے ۔ (۲) پانی جب عضو سے جدا ہو جائے تب مستعمل شمار کیا جائے۔ (۳) پانی عضو سے جدا ہو کر کسی مقام پر ، مثلا برتن میں جمع ہو جائے تب مستعمل شمار کیا جائے ۔ پانی جب عضو پر ہوتو کسی کے یہاں بھی مستعمل شمار نہیں کیا جائے گا کیونکہ اس میں ضرورت ہے۔ اگر عضو پر رہتے ہوئے مستعمل شمار کر دیا جائے تو عضو ہی کبھی پاک نہیں ہوگا۔ اور دوسری صورت کہ عضو سے جدا ہونے کے بعد فورا ہی مستعمل قرار دیا جائے کیونکہ اب ضرورت باقی نہیں رہی ۔ صحیح قول یہی ہے۔ حضرت سفیان ثوری کا قول یہ ہے کہ عضو سے جدا ہونے کے بعد کسی مقام پر ، مثلا برتن وغیرہ میں جمع ہو جائے تب جا کر پانی کو مستعمل قرار دیا جائے۔ اس سے پہلے پانی مستعمل نہیں شمار کیا جائے گا۔ صحیح کہہ کر اسی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے کہ یہ قول صحیح نہیں ہے ۔ لغت : بمجر دانفاصلہ محض جدا ہونے سے صرف جدا ہونے سے ۔ جسد جسم سے۔
(5) And water becomes used water simply by separating from the body.
Line 131, number 190) From this hadith it is known that used water is pure; only then was the water of wudu given to drink. And in Sunan al-Kubra al-Bayhaqi, chapter on the purity of used water, vol. 1, p. 1359, no. 17, many hadiths on this topic are mentioned. From these hadiths it is known that used water is pure.
And nothing can be purified with used water. For this, there are these hadiths. (1) Regarding used water not being purifying, these hadiths are used as evidence in which the Prophet took new water for each limb. If used water were purifying, he would have reused the same water and not taken new water for each limb. A hadith: From Ibn Abbas: "Do you want me to show you how the Messenger of Allah used to perform wudu? He called for a vessel containing water, scooped a handful with his right hand, rinsed his mouth and nose, then took another handful and combined his hands, then washed his face, etc." (Abu Dawud, chapter on wudu twice, p. 20, no. 137). In this hadith, separate water was taken for each limb. This would be done because the first water became used and cannot wash another limb; therefore, another water was taken for the second limb. (2) Another hadith prohibits bathing from janabah in a small amount of water. If there is no impurity on his body, the only reason for the prohibition is that after water becomes used, it cannot be used by others. Therefore, he prohibited entering it and making the water used. A hadith: Abu Hurairah said: The Messenger of Allah said: "Let none of you bathe in standing water while he is junub." (Muslim, chapter on the prohibition of bathing in standing water, p. 133, no. 658/283). From these hadiths it is known that used water is pure but does not have the capacity to purify. (3) In this athar: From Ibn Abbas regarding a man who bathes in a vessel and water splashes from that which he pours over himself into the vessel: he said: "The water is purifying but does not purify." (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on the purity of used water, vol. 1, p. 1360, no. 1122). In this statement of a Companion, used water is pure but does not have the capacity to purify. (4) From Ibrahim from Ibn Abbas that he was asked about a man who bathes or performs wudu from a vessel and water splashes into it? He said: He saw no harm in it. (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, chapter on what splashes into the vessel from wudu and ghusl, vol. 1, p. 72, no. 315). From this statement of a Companion it is also known that if used water falls into a vessel, there is no harm.
Lexicon: Tahir: the water itself is pure. Ghayr mutahhir: it is not a purifier. Li raf' al-hadath: to remove hadath, to lift hadath. Qurbah: an action done for the sake of reward.
Translation: (5) Water becomes used simply by separating from the body.
Explanation: When does water become used? There are three situations: (1) while still on the limb, it is considered used? (2) when water separates from the limb, it is considered used. (3) when water separates from the limb and collects somewhere, e.g., in a vessel, then it is considered used. When water is on the limb, it is not considered used by anyone because there is need. If it were considered used while on the limb, the limb would never become pure. The second scenario is that immediately after separation from the limb, it is considered used because the need no longer remains. This is the correct opinion. The opinion of Sufyan al-Thawri is that it is considered used only after it collects somewhere, e.g., in a vessel, after separation. Before that, it is not considered used. By saying "correct" it is indicated that this opinion is not correct.
Lexicon: Bi-mujarrad: simply by, merely by separating. Jasad: body.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on used water (musta'mal). The author states the ruling: water becomes musta'mal simply by separating from the body. The commentary then provides extensive evidence from hadith and athar to prove that used water is pure (tahir) but not purifying (ghayr mutahhir). The key distinction is that water on the limb during washing is not considered used because it is still needed for purification. Once it leaves the limb, it immediately becomes used. The commentary mentions a difference of opinion (ikhtilaf) with Sufyan al-Thawri, who held that water becomes used only after it collects in a container. The author firmly rejects this by saying the correct opinion is immediate usage. The passages also clarify the linguistic meaning of tahir and mutahhir.

Key Terms
ماء مستعمل (mā' musta'mal): Water that has been used in an act of worship (wudu/ghusl) and has separated from the body; it is pure (tahir) but not purifying (ghayr mutahhir).
طاہر (tāhir): Pure in itself; does not convey impurity but also does not remove it.
مطہر (mutahhir): Purifying; capable of removing ritual impurity (hadath) and physical impurity (najis).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, at what point does water become musta'mal (used) during wudu?
A) While it is still on the limb being washed
B) Immediately upon separating from the limb
C) Only after it collects in a vessel
D) After the entire wudu is completed

Key Takeaway
Water becomes musta'mal instantly upon separating from the body; it is tahir but not mutahhir, so it cannot be used for wudu or ghusl.

Tree and Fruit Water Invalid for Wudu
Ruling that tree and fruit water cannot be used for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using tree and fruit water for wudu and identify the operative position (mufta bihi) within the school.

Core Idea
Tree and fruit water is pure but not purifying for wudu or ghusl, even if extracted naturally.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on using water derived from trees and fruits. While such liquids are ritually pure (tahir), they are not considered mutlaq water and therefore cannot serve as the purifying agent for wudu or ghusl. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi difference regarding liquids that come out naturally versus those extracted by pressing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and prohibited substance
The act is wudu or ghusl. The substance is water from trees (e.g., sap) and fruits (e.g., juice). The ruling applies to both natural and extracted forms.
2
Understand the tahir vs mutahhir distinction
Tahir means ritually pure; mutahhir means capable of removing ritual impurity. Tree/fruit water is tahir but not mutahhir. Wudu requires mutahhir water.
This distinction is central to Hanafi fiqh.
3
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
The apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) holds that wudu with tree/fruit water is invalid in all cases, whether extracted or natural.
This is the strongest authority level.
4
Recognize the alternative view from Hidayah
Some narrations, as reflected in Al-Hidayah, suggest that if the liquid comes out naturally (without pressing), wudu may be valid. However, this view is not relied upon.
The author settles on the zahir al-riwayah position.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa is the Zahir al-Riwayah: impermissible in all cases. This is the position to follow in practice.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is resolved in favour of the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦) ولا يجوز بماء شجر و ثمر ولو خرج بنفسه من غير عصر في الاظهر.
ترجمه: (۶) درخت اور پھل کے پانی سے وضو جائز نہیں اگر چہ وہ بغیر نچوڑے ہوئے خود سے نکلا ہو، ظاہر روایت یہی ہے تشریح : درخت کا رس نکالا ہو یا خود نکلا ہو، مثلا کیلے، یا گنے کا رس نکالا ہو، یا پھل کا رس نکالا ہو، یا خود نکلا ہو مثلا ناریل کا پانی نکلا ہو تو اگر چہ یہ پاک ہے لیکن اس سے وضو کرنا یا غسل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے، اس سے آدمی پاک نہیں ہوگا۔، ظاہر روایت یہی ہے۔ ہدایہ که عبارت سے پتہ چلتا ہے کہ اگر خود بخود نکلا ہو تو اس سے وضو جائز ہے اور نکالا گیا ہو تو اس سے وضو جائز نہیں، اس لئے مصنف نے یہ فرمایا کہ ظاہر روایت یہی ہے کہ اس سے وضو جائز نہیں ہے۔
(6) And it is not permissible to perform wudu with water from trees and fruits, even if it comes out by itself without pressing, in the apparent narration.
Translation: (6) Wudu is not permissible with water from trees and fruits, even if it comes out by itself without being pressed; this is the apparent narration.
Explanation: Whether the sap of a tree is extracted or comes out by itself, e.g., banana or sugarcane juice extracted, or fruit juice extracted or comes out by itself, e.g., coconut water, even though it is pure, wudu or ghusl with it is not permissible; a person will not become pure from it. This is the apparent narration. From the text of Hidayah it appears that if it comes out by itself, wudu is permissible, and if it is extracted, wudu is not permissible. Therefore, the author said: The apparent narration is that wudu with it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that water extracted from trees and fruits (like sap, juice) cannot be used for wudu or ghusl. The author clarifies that even if the liquid comes out naturally without pressing, it is still not valid for purification in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). However, the commentary notes a nuance from al-Hidayah: if the liquid comes out by itself, some consider it permissible for wudu; if pressed, it is impermissible. The author settles on the position of the apparent narration that it is impermissible in both cases. The key point: such liquids are pure (tahir) but not mutahhir (purifying) for ritual purification.

Key Terms
ماء شجر (mā' shajar): Water extracted from trees, such as sap or juice.
ظاہر روایت (zāhir al-riwāyah): The apparent or well-known narration in the Hanafi school, as transmitted in the six major works of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on using coconut water (that comes out naturally) for wudu?
A) Permissible, because it is pure
B) Permissible, because it came out naturally
C) Not permissible, even if it came out naturally
D) Permissible only if no other water is available

Key Takeaway
Tree and fruit water cannot be used for wudu or ghusl, regardless of extraction method, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Tree and Fruit Water: Not Valid for Wudu
Statement of ruling with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using tree/fruit water for wudu and identify the Quranic evidence against its validity.

Core Idea
Tree sap and fruit juice are not mutlaq water; wudu with them is invalid even if it flows naturally.

The Hanafi school strictly defines what qualifies as 'water' for purification. This passage clarifies that liquids derived from trees or fruits—such as sap or juice—are not considered absolute water (ma' mutlaq) and therefore cannot be used for wudu or ghusl. Even if the liquid exudes naturally without pressing, the ruling remains unchanged. The evidence is drawn from the Quranic verse that conditions tayammum on the absence of water, implying only pure, unaltered water is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is performing wudu (and by extension, ghusl) with water from trees or fruits. The ruling is that it is not permissible (la yajuz), i.e., the wudu is invalid.
The phrase 'لا يجوز' indicates prohibition / invalidity.
2
Understand the condition
The ruling applies even if the liquid comes out by itself without pressing. This preempts a possible exception that natural flow might render it acceptable.
'ولو خرج بنفسه من غير عصر' = even if it exits by itself without pressing.
3
Recognize the authority level
The phrase 'في الاظهر' indicates this is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), which is the stronger transmission within the Hanafi school.
Zahir al-riwayah is the primary source of fatwa.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
There exists a weak narration that permits such water if it flows naturally. This lesson affirms that the relied-upon position is impermissibility.
The intra-school ikhtilaf is recorded but the mufta bihi is clear.
5
Grasp the proof (dalil)
The Quran (4:43) conditions tayammum on the absence of 'water' (ma'). This implies that only absolute water (ma' mutlaq) can be used for wudu; tree/fruit water is not ma' mutlaq.
The dalala is zanni but sufficiently strong for the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦) ولا يجوز بماء شجر و ثمر ولو خرج بنفسه من غير عصر في الاظهر.
(6) It is not permissible to perform wudu with water from trees or fruits, even if it comes out by itself without pressing, according to the apparent [narration].
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Hanafi school stating that water extracted from trees (like banana plant or sugarcane) or from fruits (like coconut water) is not valid for wudu or ghusl, even if it flows naturally without pressing. The reason is that such liquids are not 'absolute water' (ma' mutlaq). The apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) holds this view, while there is a narration that if it comes out by itself it is permissible, but the author affirms that the relied-upon position is impermissibility. The proof is from the Quran (Surah al-Nisa', 4:43) where Allah says 'and you do not find water, then perform tayammum,' implying that wudu requires absolute water. Also, a hadith from Abu Umamah al-Bahili states that water becomes impure only when its color, taste, or smell changes, and tree/fruit juices inherently have altered properties, so they cannot be used for purification.

Key Terms
ماء شجر (ma' shajar): Water from trees, such as sap or extract.
ماء ثمر (ma' thamar): Water from fruits, such as fruit juice.
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Absolute water that retains its original nature; the only valid water for wudu and ghusl.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or well-known transmission; the primary authoritative source in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is wudu valid with water that comes out of a coconut naturally without pressing, according to the apparent narration?
A) Yes, it is valid.
B) No, it is not valid.
C) It is valid only if pressed.
D) It is valid but makruh.

Key Takeaway
Tree and fruit water (sap, juice) are not absolute water; wudu with them is invalid in the Hanafi school.

Ruling: Water Changed by Cooking or Mixing is Not Valid for Wudu
Statement of ruling with proof from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two causes that remove water's purifying quality and explain why such water invalidates wudu.

Core Idea
Water must retain its original nature to be purifying; cooking and mixing remove that nature.

This lesson clarifies when water loses its legal status as mutahhir (purifying). Even if water remains physically pure (tahir), it may no longer be suitable for purification. The Hanafi school bases this on the Quranic description of water as 'tahuran'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing wudu or ghusl with water whose nature has changed. The ruling is that it is not valid – such water cannot be used for purification.
2
Understand the two causes
The text mentions two causes: (1) cooking something in the water, e.g., making broth; (2) dominance of another substance, e.g., adding much saffron or juice. Both alter the water's tab' (nature).
3
Apply the distinction between tahir and mutahhir
The water may still be tahir (pure) but is no longer mutahhir (purifying). This distinction is key: purity for consumption differs from purity for worship.
4
Refer to the Quranic proof
The Quran (25:48) calls water 'tahuran', implying it must be inherently purifying. Water that has lost its original nature no longer qualifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷) ولا بماء زال طبعه بالطبخ او بغلبة غيره عليه .
(7) And it is not permissible [to perform wudu] with water whose nature has been removed by cooking or by the dominance of something else over it.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This ruling explains when water becomes unsuitable for wudu or ghusl. The 'nature' (tab') of water refers to its being pure and absolute. This nature can be lost in two ways: (1) by cooking something in it, like potatoes, making it broth; (2) by mixing a substance that dominates it, like adding a large amount of saffron or juice. In such cases, even if the water is still pure (tahir), it is no longer purifying (mutahhir). However, if the change is minimal—for instance, boiling water with jujube leaves does not alter its nature—then wudu is valid. The proof is from the Quran (Surah al-Furqan, 25:48) where Allah says 'and We sent down pure water from the sky,' indicating that purifying water must be pure in essence.

Key Terms
طبعه (tab'uhu): The original nature of water as absolute and purifying.
الطبخ (al-tabkh): Cooking something in water, which changes the water's nature.
غلبة غيره (ghalabat ghayrihi): The dominance of another substance over water, causing its nature to change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is wudu valid with water in which potatoes were cooked until it became broth?
A) Yes, because it is still water.
B) No, because its nature has changed.
C) Yes, because cooking does not affect purity.
D) No, because it is now impure.

Key Takeaway
Water that has been changed by cooking or by the dominance of another substance loses its property of being purifying and cannot be used for wudu or ghusl.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Water Whose Nature Has Changed: Ruling and Proofs
Ruling with proofs on water losing mutlaq status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water whose nature has changed by cooking or mixing, identify the key proofs from Quran and hadith, and apply the principle to distinguish permissible and impermissible water for wudu.

Core Idea
If water loses its 'mutlaq' nature, even if pure, it cannot be used for wudu — only water that remains called 'water' is tahur.

This lesson establishes a critical principle in Hanafi taharah: the condition for water to be purifying (tahur) is that it must remain absolute water (mā' mutlaq) in name and nature. The passage examines two ways water loses its mutlaq status: cooking and the dominance of a pure substance. You will learn the ruling, the evidence from Quran and hadith, and the exceptions where the water remains usable.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on water whose nature has changed
Identify the general ruling: such water is physically pure (tahir) but not legally purifying (tahur). Wudu or ghusl with it is invalid (batil).
The ruling applies regardless of whether the change is due to cooking or mixing.
2
Identify the two ways nature changes
Note the two cases mentioned: (1) cooking a substance in water so that the water is no longer called water (e.g., broth), and (2) mixing a pure substance that dominates the water's properties (e.g., thick date syrup).
The key is whether the water retains its name, fluidity, and common recognition as 'water'.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Analyze Quran 4:43 ('...and you find no water, then perform tayammum...') and 25:48 ('And We send down pure water'). The first implies that only mutlaq water is meant for wudu; the second describes purifying water as that which descends unchanged.
The Hanafi interpretation is that 'water' in these verses refers to absolute, unchanged water.
4
Examine the hadith proof
Study the hadith from Abu Umamah: 'Water is not made impure by anything except what overpowers its smell, taste, and color.' Scholars extend this to pure substances: if a pure substance overpowers these properties, the water's purifying quality is lost.
This hadith is key; the Hanafi school applies it to pure additions that change the water's nature.
5
Examine additional proofs and exceptions
Review the companion Ibn Abbas forbidding wudu with milk, and the tabi'i Hasan al-Basri forbidding wudu with thick nabidh. Contrast with cases where the additive does not dominate: the Prophet's wudu with thin nabidh and ghusl with water containing traces of dough.
These show that if the water's nature remains intact, the ruling is permissibility.

الحسن يقول : لا يتوضأ بنبيذ و لا لبن (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ، ۷۶ ، فی الوضوء باللبن ، ج اول ص ۶۱ نمبر ۶۵۰ ) اس قول تابعی میں ہے کہ نبیذ سے وضو نہ کرے
اور اگر پانی میں پاک چیز ملی اور اس پر غالب نهیں آئی بلکه مغلوب رھی تو وضو جائز ھوگا۔ وجه (۱) اس حدیث سے اس کا استدلال ہے عن عبد الله بن مسعود ان رسول الله الله قال له ليلة الجن عندك طهور؟ قال لا الا شيء من نبيذ في ادارة قال ثمرة طيبة وماء طهور فتوضأ (ابن ماجہ، باب الوضوء بالنبيذ ص ۵۷ نمبر ۳۸۴ ر دار قطنی ، باب الوضوء بالنبیذ ج اول ص ۷۸ نمبر (۲۴) پانی میں کھجور ڈال کر نبیذ بنایا لیکن پانی کی طبیعت نہیں بدلی اس لئے اس سے وضو کرنا جائز قرار دیا۔ (۲) اس حدیث میں بھی ہے۔ عن ام هانی ان رسول الله الله اغتسل هو و ميمونة من اناء واحد في قصعة فيها أثر العجين ( نسائی شریف، باب ذکر الاغتسال في القصعة التي سجن فيها ، ص ۳۲ نمبر ۲۴۱) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ پانی میں آئے کا اثر تھا لیکن اتنا نہیں تھا کہ پانی کی طبیعت بدل جائے تو اس سے غسل جائز ہوا۔ (۳) اگر پانی میں کسی چیز کو ڈال کر پکا یا لیکن اس سے پانی کی طبیعت نہیں بدلی بلکہ نظافت اور صفائی میں اور نکھار آیا تو اس سے وضو جائز ہوگا، جیسے بیری کی پتی ڈال کر پکانے کے باوجود اس سے وضو اور غسل جائز ہے، اس کے لئے حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن ابن عباس عن النبي ع خر رجل من بعيره فوقص فمات فقال اغسلوه بماء وسدر وكفنوه في ثوبیہ ۔(مسلم شریف ، باب ما يفعل بالحرم اذامات ص ۳۸۴ نمبر ۲۸۹۱/۱۲۰۰۶/ ابن ماجہ شریف ، باب المحرم یموت بص ۴۴۹، نمبر۳۰۸۴)
(7) And it is not permissible to perform wudu with water whose nature has been removed by cooking or by the dominance of another substance over it. Explanation: (1) The squeezed water of a tree or fruit is not absolute water but juice, and if it is not absolute water, the ruling is to perform tayammum; do not perform wudu or ghusl with juice. The verse for this is: '...and you find no water, then perform tayammum with clean earth' (Surah al-Nisa, 4:43). This verse indicates that if there is no water, perform tayammum. (2) The color, taste, or smell of tree or fruit juice is necessarily changed, and in the hadith it is stated that if any of these is changed, wudu with it is not permissible. Therefore, wudu will not be permissible. One can deduce from this hadith: from Abu Umamah al-Bahili, he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Water is not made impure by anything except what overpowers its smell, taste, and color' (Ibn Majah, chapter on cisterns, p. 74, no. 521). ... Translation: (7) And it is not permissible to perform wudu with water whose nature has been removed by cooking or by the dominance of another substance over it. Explanation: If something is mixed into water, or something is cooked in it, what is its ruling? The principle is being stated: Principle: If the nature of water is removed and it no longer remains absolute water, even if it is pure, performing wudu or ghusl with it is not permissible. Further, they mention that the nature of water is removed in two ways: [1] When something is cooked in water so that the water no longer remains and people do not call it water, but give it another name, like if potatoes are cooked in water, it becomes broth. This is pure for eating, but wudu or ghusl with it is not permissible. However, if cooking does not remove the nature of water, then wudu or ghusl with that water is permissible, such as cooking berry leaves or soapwort grass in water; the water remains, so it is permissible to wash a corpse with it. [2] The second case is when something is mixed into water in such quantity that its nature is removed, then wudu and ghusl are not permissible. If mixing does not remove the nature, then wudu and ghusl are permissible. For example, dates are mixed into water so that the water becomes thick; wudu is not permissible because the water's nature changed. If it does not become thick but remains fluid, only the taste of dates appears, then wudu is permissible because the water's nature was not removed. ... Next, what things change the nature and what things do not; four scenarios are being mentioned. Evidence (1): The verse 'And We send down from the sky pure water' (Surah al-Furqan, 25:48) indicates that water is tahur when it is absolute water, and this water is no longer absolute; its name and nature have changed, so wudu and ghusl are not permissible, for example, syrup where so much substance is added that it is no longer called water but is called syrup in common usage. (2) There is an indication in the hadith from Abu Umamah al-Bahili who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Water is not made impure by anything except what overpowers its smell, taste, and color' (Ibn Majah, chapter on cisterns, p. 74, no. 521; Tahawi, chapter on water in which impurity falls, p. 15). Scholars have deduced from this hadith that if a pure substance (other than soil) is mixed into water and changes its smell, taste, and color, and the nature of water changes, then wudu and ghusl with it are not permissible. (3) A man asked Ibn Abbas: 'We go seeking pasture and do not find water; can we perform wudu with milk?' He said: 'No, you must perform tayammum' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 76, on wudu with milk, vol. 1, p. 61, no. 649). This saying of a companion indicates that wudu with milk is not permissible because it does not have the fluidity and flow of water. (4) If nabidh (date infusion) becomes thick due to the dates, then wudu with it is not permissible. There is an athar: Al-Hasan said: 'Do not perform wudu with nabidh or milk' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 76, on wudu with milk, vol. 1, p. 61, no. 650). This saying of a tabi'i indicates that one should not perform wudu with nabidh. And if a pure substance is mixed into water and it does not dominate but is dominated, then wudu is permissible. Evidence (1): This is deduced from the hadith of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said to him on the night of the jinn: 'Do you have water for purification?' He said: 'No, except some nabidh in a vessel.' He said: 'It is a good fruit and pure water, so perform wudu' (Ibn Majah, chapter on wudu with nabidh, p. 57, no. 384; Dar Qutni, chapter on wudu with nabidh, vol. 1, p. 78, no. 24). Dates were placed in water to make nabidh, but the nature of the water did not change, so wudu was deemed permissible. (2) This is also in the hadith of Umm Hani: 'The Messenger of Allah performed ghusl with Maimuna from a single vessel in a bowl that had traces of dough' (Nasa'i, chapter on mention of ghusl in a bowl in which there was dough, p. 32, no. 241). This hadith indicates that there was a trace of dough in the water, but not enough to change the nature of the water, so ghusl was permissible. (3) If something is cooked in water but the nature of the water does not change, rather it becomes clearer and cleaner, then wudu is permissible, such as cooking berry leaves; despite cooking, wudu and ghusl are permissible. The hadith for this is: Ibn Abbas narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said regarding a man who fell from his camel and died: 'Wash him with water and sidr (lotus leaves) and shroud him in his two garments' (Muslim, chapter on what to do with a Muhrim who dies, p. 384, no. 2891/12006/; Ibn Majah, chapter on the Muhrim who dies, p. 449, no. 3084). This hadith indicates...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs regarding water that has lost its 'mutlaq' (absolute) status due to either cooking or the dominance of a pure substance. The author states that such water, although physically pure (tahir), is no longer purifying (tahur), meaning wudu or ghusl with it is invalid. The ruling is based on several evidences: (1) The Quranic verse that commands tayammum when no water is found, implying that only mutlaq water qualifies for wudu. (2) The well-known hadith that water becomes impure only when its smell, taste, or color changes; scholars extend this to pure substances: if a pure substance changes these properties, the water's purifying quality is lost. (3) The companion Ibn Abbas forbade wudu with milk, analogizing that any non-water liquid that lacks the fluidity of water cannot substitute. (4) The tabi'i Hasan al-Basri forbade wudu with thick nabidh (date infusion). Conversely, the passage also provides proofs for when the added substance does not dominate: (1) The Prophet performed wudu with nabidh that was still thin and watery, showing that if the water's nature remains, it is permissible. (2) The Prophet's ghusl with water that had traces of dough—insufficient to change its nature. (3) The Prophet's instruction to wash a deceased Muhrim with water and sidr leaves, showing that if the additive does not change the water's essence, it remains purifying. The key principle is: water remains purifying (tahur) as long as it retains its name, nature, and fluidity. If it becomes a different substance in common parlance (like broth, syrup, or thick nabidh), it is no longer suitable for ritual purification.

Key Terms
ماء مطلق (mā' mutlaq): Absolute water; pure natural water that has not been mixed with anything that changes its name, nature, or properties.
طهور (tahūr): Purifying; water that is both physically pure (tahir) and legally capable of removing ritual impurity (hadath).
طبع (tab'): Nature or quality of water; its fluidity, name, and the fact that it is called 'water' in common usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following water types is NOT permissible for wudu according to this passage?
A) Water that has a slight taste of dates but remains fluid
B) Water that has been cooked with soapwort grass and remains clear
C) Water that has become thick (gādha) due to mixing with dates
D) Water that has a trace of dough but its nature is unchanged

Key Takeaway
Water remains purifying only as long as it retains its original nature, name, and fluidity; any change that removes 'mutlaq' status invalidates its use for wudu.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
Rule When Pure Substance Changes Water's Nature
States main ruling and provides three proofs from hadith and athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water changed by a pure substance and identify the three proofs that support it.

Core Idea
Water that loses its absolute nature due to a pure substance becomes non-purifying, even if not impure.

This passage establishes a key rule in Hanafi fiqh: only absolute water (ma' mutlaq) is purifying. When a pure substance mixes into water and overpowers its name, smell, taste, or color, the water ceases to be tahur—even though it remains pure (tahir). The commentator presents three evidentiary proofs: a hadith from Abu Umamah, a statement of Ibn Abbas, and a statement of Hasan al-Basri. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly identifying which waters are valid for wudu and ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the opening sentence: water that is not ma' mutlaq cannot be used for wudu or ghusl. The example of syrup clarifies that the water's name and nature have changed.
The key term is 'ghayr tahur'—the water is pure but no longer purifying.
2
Examine the first proof (hadith)
The hadith from Abu Umamah states that water is not rendered impure except by something that overcomes its smell, taste, and color. Hanafis apply this logic to pure substances via istihsan.
The hadith explicitly mentions najasah, but the principle extends to dominant pure substances.
3
Analyze the second proof (qawl sahabi)
Ibn Abbas forbade wudu with milk and ordered tayammum instead, because milk lacks the fluidity of water. This shows that a pure liquid can lose the property of being purifying.
The rationale (illah) is the absence of raqat (fluidity) and sayalan (flow).
4
Review the third proof (qawl tabi'i)
Hasan al-Basri forbade wudu with nabidh (thick date drink) and milk. This reinforces that thick or altered liquids are not suitable for purification.
The thickness (ghilaz) of nabidh indicates dominance of the added substance.
5
Synthesize the three proofs
All three evidences converge on the same point: when a pure substance changes the water's name, nature, or any of its three properties, the water is no longer tahur.
This is the established mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school.

(مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ، ۷۶، فی الوضوء باللبن ، ج اول ص ۶۱ نمبر ۶۴۹) اس قول صحابی میں ہے کہ دودھ سے وضو جائز نہیں ہے اسلئے کہ اس میں رقت اور سیلان پانی کی طرح نہیں ہے۔ (۴) جس نبیذ میں کھجور کا جز اتنا آ گیا ہو کہ وہ گاڑھا ہو گیا ہو تو اس سے وضو کرنا جائز نہیں ہے، اس کے لئے اثریہ ہے۔ سمع عن الحسن يقول : لا يتوضأ بنبيذ و لا لبن (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ، ۷۶ ، فی الوضوء باللبن ، ج اول ص ۶۱ نمبر ۶۵۰ ) اس قول تابعی میں ہے کہ نبیذ سے وضو نہ کرے
In the book of Taharah: The water that is tahur is absolute water (ma' mutlaq). This water has ceased to be absolute; its name has changed and its nature has changed, therefore wudu and ghusl are not permissible with it. For example, syrup: so much substance has been mixed into it that it is no longer called water, rather in common usage it is called syrup. (2) There is an indication of this in the hadith: From Abu Umamah al-Bahili that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Water is not made impure by anything except what overcomes its smell, taste, and color.' (Ibn Majah, Kitab al-Hiyad, p.74, no.521; Tahawi, Bab al-Ma' Taqa' fihi al-Najasah, p.15) Scholars have deduced from this hadith that if a pure substance, other than earth, mixes with water and changes its smell, taste, and color, and the nature of the water changes, then wudu and ghusl are not permissible with it. (3) A man asked Ibn Abbas: 'We go out to seek vegetation and do not find water, so should we perform wudu with milk?' He said: 'No, do tayammum.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 76, Fi al-Wudu' bi al-Laban, vol.1, p.61, no.649) This statement of a Companion indicates that wudu with milk is not permissible because it does not have the fluidity and flow of water. (4) Nabidh in which so much date component has been added that it has become thick: wudu with it is not permissible. There is a narration for this: He heard from Hasan (al-Basri) saying: 'Do not perform wudu with nabidh nor with milk.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 76, Fi al-Wudu' bi al-Laban, vol.1, p.61, no.650) This statement of a Tabi'i indicates that one should not perform wudu with nabidh.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational ruling that water which loses its absolute nature (mutlaqiyyah) due to the admixture of a pure substance becomes non-purifying (ghayr tahur). The commentator opens by stating the ruling clearly: only absolute water (ma' mutlaq) is tahur (purifying). Once water's name and nature change—like becoming syrup—it can no longer be used for wudu or ghusl. Three evidentiary layers are then provided. The first is a hadith from Abu Umamah al-Bahili: water is not rendered impure (najis) except by something that overcomes its smell, taste, or color. Hanafi scholars understand this to include pure substances that alter the water's properties, making it non-purifying even though it is not najis. The second proof is a statement of Ibn Abbas (a qawl sahabi) categorically rejecting wudu with milk and ordering tayammum instead. The commentator notes the rationale: milk lacks the fluidity (raqat) and flow (sayalan) characteristic of water. The third proof is a statement of Hasan al-Basri (a qawl tabi'i) forbidding wudu with thick nabidh (date drink) and milk. Together, these evidences establish that when a pure substance dominates the water, changing its identity, the water's purifying property is lost.

Key Terms
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Water that is naturally occurring and unchanged by any substance; it retains its absolute nature.
طهور (tahur): Purifying water—water that is itself pure and can purify other things (for wudu, ghusl, removing najasah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The water must be free of any impurity.
B) The water must not have its name or nature changed by a pure substance.
C) The water must be from a well or river.
D) The water must be free of any color or taste.

Key Takeaway
A pure substance can render water non-purifying if it changes the water's name, nature, or any of its three properties.

When a Pure Substance Does Not Dominate Water
Ruling with four proofs from hadith and athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water mixed with a non-dominant pure substance and identify the four proofs used to support this ruling.

Core Idea
Water mixed with a pure substance remains tahur as long as its fluidity and flow are unchanged.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle: a pure substance (tahir) that mixes with water but does not dominate (ghalaba) does not remove the water's purifying quality. The passage presents four evidences from hadith and athar. By working through these proofs, you will understand the school's reasoning and the practical criterion of fluidity (raqat) and flow (sayalan).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the precise legal position: wudu and ghusl are valid with water mixed with a pure substance, as long as the substance does not dominate the water.
The ruling is based on the principle that water remains tahur unless its nature changes.
2
Understand the criterion of dominance
Define ghalaba (dominance) as the substance overcoming the water's properties, especially loss of fluidity (raqat) and flow (sayalan). If water remains liquid and free-flowing, it is valid.
Hanafis emphasize raqat; Shafi'is consider change in any of the three properties (taste, smell, color).
3
Examine the four proofs
Analyze each dalil: 1) Nabidh hadith (Ibn Majah) – the Prophet called it 'good fruit and purifying water'; 2) Umm Hani' hadith (Nasa'i) – ghusl in a bowl with dough traces; 3) Sidr hadith (Muslim) – washing a deceased muhrim with water and ber leaves; 4) Ibn Mas'ud's athar (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) – washing head with khitami.
Each proof illustrates that the water's nature did not change despite admixture.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some Shafi'is hold that any change in one property (smell, taste, color) invalidates the water, even if it remains liquid. The Hanafi school differs, requiring loss of fluidity for invalidity.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in ruling.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., adding a few drops of rose water to a bucket), determine validity using the Hanafi criterion: is the water still liquid and free-flowing? If yes, wudu is valid.
Focus on the water's physical state, not just the presence of the substance.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if a pure substance mixes with water and does not dominate it, rather it is overcome (maghlub), then wudu is valid. Proof (1): It is deduced from this hadith: From Abdullah ibn Mas'ud that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said to him on the night of the jinn: 'Do you have purifying water?' He said, 'No, except some nabidh in a container.' He said, 'A good fruit and purifying water,' and he performed wudu. (Ibn Majah, Bab al-Wudu' bi al-Nabidh, p.57, no.384; Dar Qutni, Bab al-Wudu' bi al-Nabidh, vol.1, p.78, no.24) Dates were put in water to make nabidh, but the nature of the water did not change, so wudu with it was deemed permissible. (2) Also in this hadith: From Umm Hani' that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) performed ghusl with Maymunah from a single vessel in a bowl that had traces of dough. (Nasa'i, Bab Dhikr al-Ightisal fi al-Qas'ah, p.32, no.241) This hadith indicates that there were traces of dough in the water, but not enough to change its nature, so ghusl with it was valid. (3) If something is added to water and boiled, but the nature of the water does not change; rather, it becomes clearer for cleanliness and purity, then wudu with it is valid. For example, using ber leaf (sidr) added to water and boiled: wudu and ghusl with it are valid. There is a hadith for this: From Ibn Abbas from the Prophet (peace be upon him): a man fell from his camel and died, so he said, 'Wash him with water and sidr (ber leaves) and shroud him in his two garments.' (Muslim, Bab ma Yuf'al bi al-Haram idha Mata, p.384, no.2891/12006; Ibn Majah, Bab al-Muhrim Yamut, p.449, no.3084) From this hadith, it is learned that if ber leaf or something else is mixed into water for cleaning, then achieving purification with that water is permissible, provided that its fluidity (raqat) and flow (sayalan) have not ceased. (4) There is also evidence in this narration: He said, 'I heard Ibn Mas'ud saying: Any junub who washes his head with khitami (marsh mallow) has done well.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-Rajul Yaghsulu Ra'sahu bi al-Sidr, vol.1, p.203, no.1007) In this narration, water mixed with khitami was used for washing, yet ghusl was still valid.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario when a pure substance mixes with water but does not overcome it (maghlub). The commentator presents four pieces of evidence demonstrating that such water remains valid for purification. The first is the well-known hadith of the night of the jinn, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) used nabidh (date water) that had not changed the water's nature; he called it 'a good fruit and purifying water,' showing that wudu with it was allowed. The second is the hadith of Umm Hani': the Prophet and Maymunah used a bowl that had traces of dough (athar al-'ajin), yet that did not prevent ghusl, as the water's nature was unchanged. The third proof is the hadith ordering the washing of a muhrim (pilgrim in ihram) who died, with water and sidr (ber leaves). The commentator explains that ber leaves are added for cleaning, not to change the water's nature; as long as fluidity (raqat) and flow (sayalan) remain, wudu and ghusl are valid. The fourth evidence is the statement of Ibn Mas'ud permitting a junub to wash his head with khitami (marsh mallow) mixed in water. All these evidences converge on the principle: a pure substance that does not dominate (ghalaba) and does not remove the water's fluidity and flow does not affect the water's purifying quality.

Key Terms
غالب (ghalib): Dominant; a substance that overcomes the water's properties (smell, taste, color, fluidity) and changes its nature.
رقت (raqat): Fluidity; the liquid state of water that allows it to flow. Loss of raqat indicates the substance has dominated.
طهور (tahur): Purifying; water that is itself pure and can be used to purify others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which condition makes water mixed with a pure substance still valid for wudu?
A) The substance must be a fruit.
B) The water must remain liquid and its nature unchanged.
C) The water must be heated.
D) The substance must be completely dissolved.

Key Takeaway
A pure substance that does not dominate water (ghalaba) does not affect the water's purifying quality; the water remains tahur as long as its fluidity and flow are intact.

Criteria for Ghalaba: Solids vs. Liquids in Water
Classifies pure substances and defines ghalaba for solids and liquids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a pure substance has dominated water by applying the Hanafi criteria: for solids, check fluidity and flow; for liquids, count the changed qualities or weight increase.

Core Idea
For solids, dominance means the water no longer flows; for liquids, it means a specified number of properties change.

Imagine you add saffron to your wudu water. It turns yellow but still pours easily. Is your wudu valid? The Hanafi school gives a precise answer based on whether the added substance 'dominates' the water. This lesson breaks down the classification of pure substances and the exact criteria for each type, as taught by the classical scholars.

Guided Path
1
State the overarching principle
The author begins: if the water's nature (tabi'at) is unchanged, wudu is valid; if changed, invalid. This is the foundation for all subsequent classifications.
2
Identify the four categories of pure substances
From the table: (1) solids (jamid), (2) liquids with two qualities (e.g., color and taste), (3) liquids with three qualities (color, taste, smell), (4) liquids with no inherent qualities.
3
Understand the criterion for solids
For solids, dominance (ghalaba) occurs only when the water loses its raqat (fluidity) and sayalan (flow). This is deduced from the hadith forbidding wudu with thick nabidh and the permissibility with dough traces.
The solid itself does not dominate by merely changing color or taste.
4
Analyze the evidence for solids
The author cites Hasan al-Basri's statement 'لا يتوضأ بنبيذ و لا لبن' (do not perform wudu with nabidh nor milk) because nabidh becomes thick from dates, removing fluidity. Contrast with the hadith of Umm Hani' where traces of dough remained but water still flowed, so ghusl was valid.
This confirms that fluidity is the key indicator for solids.
5
Distinguish from other madhahib
Other schools may consider any change in property (color, taste, smell) as dominance. The Hanafi specificity for solids raises the bar: only when water becomes thick and loses flow is it non-purifying.
This is a practical difference: e.g., yellow saffron water still purifies in the Hanafi school.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول: پکانے سے ، یا اجز املانے سے پانی کی طبیعت ن بدلی ہو تو وضو جائز ہے، اور طبیعت بدل گئی ہو تو وضو جائز نہیں۔ پانی میں کوئی چیزل جائے تواس کے احکام ایک نظر میں نمبر ملنے کی قسمیں (۱) پکانا شوربے کے لئے وضو جائز نہیں (۲) پکانا نظافت کے لئے وضو جائز ہے (۳) منجمد شی کا ملنا وضو جائز نہیں (۴) رقیق شی ء کا ملنا جس کے صرف دو وصف ہوں ایک وصف سے غلبہ ہوگا (۵) رقیق شی کا ملنا جس کے تین وصف ہوں دو وصف سے غلبہ ہو گا (1) رقیق شی ء کا ملنا جس کا کوئی وصف نہ ہو وزن کے بڑھنے سے غلبہ ہوگا ترجمه (۸) اور پانی میں منجمد اشیاء کے ملنے سے جب پانی کی رقت اور سیلان زائل ہو جائے تو غیر شی ء کا غلبہ کہا جائے گا۔ تشریح : چار قسم کی چیزیں ہیں ، جب یہ پانی میں مل جائیں تو ہر ایک کے غلبے کی صورت الگ الگ ہے، اس کی تفصیل بیان کی جارہی ہے [1] پہلی چیز ۔ منجمد چیزیں ، جیسے زعفران ، پھل، درخت کی پتی پانی میں مل جائے، تو اس وقت اس کو پانی پر غالب سمجھا جائے گا جب پانی گاڑھا ہو جائے اور پانی کی رقیت ختم ہو جائے اور سیلان میں کمی آجائے۔ وجه : (۱) اس اثر میں ہے کہ نبیذ سے وضو نہ کرے اس لئے کہ وہ گاڑھا ہے جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ جامد ملنے رقیت اور سیلان ختم ہو جائے تو وضو جائز نہیں ہے۔ قول تابعی یہ ہے . سمع عن الحسن يقول : لا يتوضأ بنبيذ و لا لبن (مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ،۷۶، فی الوضوء باللبن ، ج اول ص ۶۱ نمبر ۶۵۰) اس قول تابعی میں ہے کہ نبیذ سے وضو نہ کرے کیونکہ کھجور ملنے کی وجہ سے گاڑھا ہو چکا ہے۔ (۲) اور اس حدیث میں آنے کی وجہ سے رقت اور سیلان باقی ہے اس لئے غسل جائز ہوا۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن ام هانی ان رسول الله الله الله اغتسل هو و ميمونة من اناء واحد في قصعة فيها أثر العجين ( نسائی شریف، باب ذکر
Principle: If water's nature does not change due to boiling or mixing parts, then wudu is permissible. If its nature does change, wudu is not permissible. When something is added to water, its rulings at a glance: Types of mixtures (1) Boiling for broth: wudu not permissible. (2) Boiling for cleaning: wudu permissible. (3) Solid substance mixing: wudu not permissible. (4) Liquid substance mixing that has only two descriptions: dominance by one description. (5) Liquid substance mixing that has three descriptions: dominance by two descriptions. (6) Liquid substance mixing that has no description: dominance by increase in weight. Translation of (8): And when solid substances mix with water and its fluidity (raqat) and flow (sayalan) are removed, then the other substance is said to be dominant. Explanation: There are four types of things; when they mix with water, the manner of dominance for each is different. The details are being explained. [1] First type: Solid things, such as saffron, fruits, tree leaves mixed in water. At that time, it will be considered dominant over water when the water becomes thick and its fluidity is removed and its flow decreases. Evidence: (1) In this narration, it says not to perform wudu with nabidh because it is thick. From this it is known that when a solid mixes and removes fluidity and flow, wudu is not permissible. The statement of the Tabi'i is: He heard from Hasan (al-Basri) saying: 'Do not perform wudu with nabidh nor with milk.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 76, Fi al-Wudu' bi al-Laban, vol.1, p.61, no.650) This statement of the Tabi'i indicates that one should not perform wudu with nabidh because it has become thick due to the dates. (2) And in this hadith, because fluidity and flow remain, ghusl was valid. The hadith is: From Umm Hani' that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) performed ghusl with Maymunah from a single vessel in a bowl that had traces of dough. (Nasa'i, Bab Dhikr
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage provides a systematic classification of pure substances that may mix with water and the criteria for determining when they dominate (ghalaba) the water, rendering it non-purifying. The author first states the overarching principle: if water's nature (tabi'at) does not change, wudu is valid; if it changes, not valid. Then a summary table lists five categories: (1) boiling for broth (e.g., cooking meat) – wudu invalid; (2) boiling for cleaning (e.g., adding ber leaves) – wudu valid; (3) solid substance mixing – wudu invalid because it thickens water; (4) liquid substance with two descriptive qualities (e.g., color and taste) – dominance by one quality suffices; (5) liquid substance with three qualities – dominance by two; (6) liquid with no inherent quality – dominance determined by weight increase. After the table, the author elaborates on the first type: solid (jamid) substances like saffron, fruits, leaves. The determinant of dominance for solids is the removal of water's fluidity (raqat) and flow (sayalan). Proof is given from the statement of Hasan al-Basri forbidding wudu with thick nabidh: it became thick from dates (a solid). The contrasting evidence is the hadith of Umm Hani', where traces of dough (a solid) remained but did not remove fluidity, so ghusl was valid. This shows that the presence of a solid alone does not invalidate; only when it removes fluidity does the water become non-purifying.

Key Terms
جامد (jamid): Solid substance; one that does not flow on its own, e.g., saffron, fruits, leaves.
رقیق (raqiq): Liquid substance; one that flows, e.g., milk, juice. For such substances, dominance is determined by the number of descriptive qualities (awsaf) or weight.
غلبة (ghalaba): Dominance: the condition where the added substance overcomes the water such that it loses its purifying property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines whether a solid substance (jamid) has dominated water?
A) The water changes color.
B) The water changes taste.
C) The water loses its fluidity and flow.
D) The water's weight increases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi criterion for solid dominance is loss of water's fluidity and flow, not mere change in color, taste, or smell.

The Domination of Solids: When Does Water Lose Its Purifying Property?
Ruling with evidence on solid-water mixture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for when a solid substance dominates water and apply it to determine the validity of wudu and ghusl.

Core Idea
Water remains purifying with solids until it loses fluidity and flow; only then is it dominated.

In this passage, the author explains the Hanafi position on mixing solid substances with water for purification. Unlike the Shafi'i school, which looks at changes in color, taste, or smell, Hanafis focus on a single criterion: the loss of water's fluidity (raqah) and flowability (sayalan). This principle is supported by evidence from the practice of the Companions and early scholars. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying taharah rulings in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Using water mixed with solids for wudu/ghusl. The ruling: valid if fluidity and flow remain; invalid if they are lost.
2
Locate the dalil
Evidence from the athar of Ibn Mas'ud (washing head with marshmallow) and the saying of Hasan al-Basri (forbidding wudu with nabidh).
Both are zanni al-thubut but accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the evidence prove the ruling? The athar shows that water with a solid (marshmallow) is still pure for ghusl; the saying shows that thickened water (nabidh) is not valid for wudu.
The phrase 'قد أبلغ' indicates sufficiency.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Shafi'is use change in any attribute (color, taste, smell) as the criterion; Hanafis restrict to loss of fluidity/flow.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position is that fluidity and flow are the decisive factors, as supported by the evidence.
Mufta bihi: water remains valid as long as raqah and sayalan persist.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with evidence on the effect of mixing solid substances (jamidat) with water for purification. The matn states the key principle: domination (ghalabah) of a solid over water is determined by the removal of water's fluidity (raqah) and flowability (sayalan). The commentary clarifies that as long as water remains thin and flowing, it is still mutlaq and valid for wudu and ghusl, even if its color, taste, or smell change. Evidence is cited from the practice of the Companions and Tabi'un. The athar from Ibn Mas'ud shows that washing the head with marshmallow (a solid) still constituted sufficient ghusl. The saying of Hasan al-Basri forbids wudu with nabidh (date infusion) because it becomes thick. A hadith from Umm Hani' shows that water with traces of dough (a solid) was still used for ghusl, indicating that as long as the water's flow remains, it is pure. The table summarizes various mixing scenarios: cooking for broth renders water non-valid, but cooking for cleanliness may keep it valid; solids cause invalidity only when fluidity is lost; liquids are judged by change in attributes. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is that water mixed with solids remains pure for purification unless it becomes thick and loses its fluidity and flow.

Key Terms
جامدات (jamidat): Solid substances such as saffron, fruits, leaves that mix with water.
رقة (raqah): Fluidity or thinness of water; its opposite is thickness (ghilaz).
سيان (sayalan): Flowability; the property of water to flow freely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the water's taste changes
C) When the water loses its fluidity (raqah) and flow (sayalan)
D) When any attribute of water changes

Key Takeaway
Solids dominate water only when they remove its fluidity and flow; as long as water remains thin and flowing, it is valid for purification.

Taharah — Purification

Change in Attributes by Solids Does Not Harm: The Hanafi Criterion of Fluid and Flow
Ruling that all three descriptions may change by solids without harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water whose three descriptions change due to solids, and explain why fluidity and flow are the decisive criteria.

Core Idea
Change in water's color, taste, or smell from solids does not invalidate it; only loss of fluidity and flow makes it unusable.

You might have seen pond water turn brown or green from leaves, or water colored by saffron. Does that make it impure for wudu? The Hanafi school offers a clear and practical criterion: as long as the water remains thin and flows, it is still pure. This passage from Thamaratun Najah explains the principle and gives examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? Using water for wudu or ghusl. What is the ruling? It is permissible (sahih) even if all three descriptions (color, taste, smell) have changed due to solids.
The ruling applies as long as water retains its fluidity (raqah) and flow (sayalan).
2
Locate the decisive condition
Find the condition that determines validity: water must remain in a state of raqah (thinness) and sayalan (flow). If these are gone, the water becomes dominated (maghlub) by the solid and is no longer valid.
This is the core principle: ghalabah (domination) is measured by loss of fluidity, not by change in attributes.
3
Apply the principle to examples
Consider the examples given: saffron, fruits, tree leaves. They change color, taste, or smell but do not thicken the water. Therefore, water remains valid. Contrast with a scenario where a solid dissolves and makes the water thick (e.g., starch) – then wudu is invalid.
The principle distinguishes between solids that merely alter attributes and those that change the water's consistency.
4
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
Note that other schools like the Shafi'i consider any change in the three descriptions as making water non-purifying. The Hanafi view is more lenient in this regard.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is significant in everyday life, especially in rural areas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹) ولا يضـر تـغيـر اوصافه كله بجامد كزعفران و فاكهة وورق شجر ترجمه: (۹) اور منجمد چیزوں سے تمام اوصاف بھی بدل جائے تب بھی کوئی حرج نہیں ہے، جیسے زعفران، اور میوے، اور درخت کے پتے گرنے سے ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح : جامد چیز مثلا زعفران ، پھل، یا درخت کا پتہ پانی میں گر جائے تو چاہے اس سے پانی کے تینوں اوصاف [ رنگ، بو، مزا] بدل جائے تب بھی وضو کرنا جائز ہے ، جب تک کہ پانی کی رقت اور سیلان نہ بدل جائے ۔ دیہات کے تالاب میں پتہ گر کر پانی کا رنگ، مزا، اور بو بدل جاتا ہے لیکن رقت اور سیلان باقی رہتی اس لئے لوگ اس کو وضو اور غسل کے لئے استعمال کرتے رہتے ہیں ۔ جامد چیزوں میں غلبے کا اعتبار رقت اور سیلان کے بدلنے سے ہے ، رنگ ، بواور مزا بدلنے سے نہیں ہے۔ اصول : جامد شی ء سے پانی کی رقت اور سیلان ختم ہو جائے تو پانی مغلوب ہو گیا اور وہ شی ء غالب ہوگئی اس لئے اب اس سے وضو کرنا جائز نہیں ۔ اور رقت اور سیلان ہے لیکن اوصاف ثلاثہ بدل گئے تو ابھی پانی پر دوسری شی کا غلبہ نہیں ہے، وضو جائز ہے۔ لغت : اوصاف وصف کی جمع ہے، رنگ، بو، مزا کو اوصاف کہتے ہیں، بو : خوشبو، اور بد بو کو بو کہتے ہیں۔ فاکھہ : میوہ ، پھل، جیسے کھجور، انگور، انار و غیره - لون : رنگ طعم : مزا۔ رحیہ خوشبو، یا بد بو ۔
(9) And change of all its descriptions by solids does not harm, like saffron, fruit, and tree leaves. Translation: (9) And if all descriptions are also changed by solids, even then there is no harm, as when saffron, fruits, or tree leaves fall in. Explanation: If a solid thing like saffron, fruit, or tree leaf falls into water, then even if all three descriptions [color, smell, taste] of water change, wudu is still permissible, as long as the water's fluidity and flow have not changed. In village ponds, a leaf falls and changes water's color, taste, and smell, but fluidity and flow remain, so people continue to use it for wudu and ghusl. In solids, domination is based on change of fluidity and flow, not on change of color, smell, or taste. Principle: If a solid removes water's fluidity and flow, then water is dominated and that thing has dominated, so wudu from it is not permissible. And if fluidity and flow remain but the three descriptions change, then the other thing has not yet dominated water; wudu is permissible. Lexicon: 'awṣāf' is plural of 'waṣf'; color, smell, taste are called descriptions. 'Rīḥa': fragrance or stench. 'Fākiha': fruit, like dates, grapes, pomegranates, etc. 'Lown': color. 'Ṭa'm': taste.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (number 9) that clarifies the scope of the previous principle: even if all three descriptions (color, taste, smell) of water change due to solid substances like saffron, fruits, or leaves, the water remains valid for wudu and ghusl. The key condition is that the water's fluidity (raqah) and flow (sayalan) must remain unchanged. The commentary gives the example of pond water in villages where leaves change the color, taste, and smell, yet people continue to use it for purification because it has not lost its thinness and flow. The underlying rationale is that domination (ghalabah) of a solid is determined solely by the loss of fluidity and flow, not by changes in the three descriptions. This is a distinct Hanafi position; other schools may consider any change in the three descriptions as altering the water's status. The linguistic section defines key terms: awsaf (descriptions: color, smell, taste), laon (color), ta'm (taste), rihah (fragrance or odor).

Key Terms
اوصاف ثلاثہ (awsaf thalathah): The three descriptions of water: color (laun), taste (ta'm), and smell (rihah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a leaf falls into a pond and changes the water's color, taste, and smell, what is the Hanafi ruling on using that water for wudu?
A) It is not permissible because three descriptions changed
B) It is permissible only if the leaf is removed
C) It is permissible because the water's fluidity and flow remain
D) It is permissible only for ghusl, not wudu

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, water remains pure for wudu and ghusl even if its color, taste, or smell change due to solids, as long as its fluidity and flow are not lost.

Domination of Liquids: Ghalabah by One Attribute for Two-Attribute Liquids
Ruling on domination (ghalabah) of two-attribute liquids in water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when a liquid with two attributes dominates water and apply it to determine the validity of wudu.

Core Idea
For liquids with only two attributes, a change in one attribute suffices to indicate domination, invalidating water for purification.

This lesson explains a key Hanafi principle in taharah: when a liquid such as milk (which has only color and taste) mixes with water, mere change in one attribute is enough to establish ghalabah (domination). Consequently, the water becomes unsuitable for wudu. This rule highlights the focus on attributes rather than volume or fluidity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the liquid's attributes
Determine how many attributes (wasf) the liquid has. For milk: color and taste, no smell.
Liquids may have two or three attributes; this rule applies only to the two-attribute case.
2
Apply the ghalabah rule for two-attribute liquids
If the liquid has two attributes, domination is established when one attribute (color or taste) changes in the water.
This differs from three-attribute liquids where two must change.
3
Determine the ruling on wudu
If domination is established, the water is no longer pure (mutahhir) and wudu with it is invalid.
The water is considered dominated by the liquid, losing its purifying property.
4
Distinguish from liquids with three attributes
Recognize that this lesson covers only two-attribute liquids; for three attributes, two attributes must change for ghalabah.
This distinction is crucial for applying the correct rule in different cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰) والغلبة في المائعات ترجمه: (۱۰) اور بہنے والی چیز میں صرف ایک وصف کے بدلنے سے غلبہ شمار ہو گا اگر اسکے صرف دو وصف ہوں ، مثلا دودھ، کہ اس میں صرف رنگ اور مزا ہے اور بو نہیں ہے۔ تشریح : یہ غلبہ کی دوسری قسم ہے بہنے والی چیز میں دو وصف ہوں تو صرف ایک وصف پانی پر غالب ہو جائے تو اس سے اس شی کا غلبہ شمار کیا جائے گا ، اور وضو جائز نہیں ہو گا۔ مثلا دودھ میں صرف دو صفت ہیں ، رنگ ، اور مزا، بونہیں ہے ، اس لئے پانی میں

Translation: (10) And in a flowing thing, domination will be counted by change of one attribute if it has only two attributes, for example milk, which has only color and taste and no smell. Explanation: This is the second type of domination. When a liquid has two attributes, if one attribute dominates over water, then domination of that thing will be counted, and wudu will not be permissible. For example, milk has only two qualities: color and taste, no smell. Therefore, in water...
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (number 10) that addresses the domination (ghalabah) of liquid substances (ma'i'at) when mixed with water. The matn itself is very brief: 'And domination in liquids.' The commentary explains that for liquids with only two attributes (like milk: color and taste, but no smell), if even one attribute changes the water, that indicates the liquid has dominated, and wudu with that water becomes impermissible. The reasoning is that such liquids lack a third attribute, so change in one is sufficient to show ghalabah. The passage begins to elaborate on other cases (liquids with three attributes require two attributes to change, etc.) but is cut off in the OCR. This is a distinct Hanafi principle: liquids are judged by their attributes, unlike solids which are judged by fluidity and flow.

Key Terms
مائعات (ma'i'at): Flowing liquids, as opposed to solids (jamidat). Examples: milk, oil, vinegar.
وصف (wasf): Attribute or quality of a liquid. For milk: color and taste. For other liquids: color, taste, and smell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Ghalabah: When Mixed Water Becomes Impure
States the legal threshold for when mixed water becomes impure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi criteria for determining ghalabah and apply them to classify a water sample as pure or impure for wudu.

Core Idea
Water becomes impure when the added substance's attributes or weight dominate according to specific rules.

Water mixed with another liquid is not automatically impure. The Hanafi school defines clear thresholds based on how many attributes the added liquid has. This lesson lays out the exact rules for ghalabah, helping you distinguish between water that remains pure (mutlaq) and water that becomes impure (najis) and unfit for wudu or ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of added liquid
Determine how many attributes (color, taste, smell) the added liquid normally possesses.
Examples: milk has two (color, taste); vinegar has three; used water has none.
2
Apply the attribute rule
For a two-attribute liquid: if one attribute appears in the water, it overcomes. For a three-attribute liquid: if two attributes appear, it overcomes.
The mixture becomes impure only when the specified number of attributes appear.
3
Apply the weight rule for attribute-less liquids
If the added liquid has no attributes (e.g., used water, scentless rosewater), compare weights. If the added liquid weighs more than half the total mixture, it overcomes.
Weight is decisive when attributes are absent.
4
Decide the water's purity status
If the added liquid overcomes (by attributes or weight), the mixture is impure and cannot be used for wudu. Otherwise, the water remains pure (mutlaq) and suitable for purification.
The ruling is binary: impure or pure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بظهور وصف واحد من مائع له وصفان فقط كاللبن له اللون و الطعم ولا رائحة له وبظهور وصفين من مائع له ثلاثة كالخل والغلبة في المائع الذي لا وصف له كالماء المستعمل وماء الورد المنقطع الرائحة تكون بالوزن فان اختلط رطلان من المستعمل برطل من المطلق لا يجوز به الوضوء وبعكسه جاز
By the appearance of one description from a liquid that has only two descriptions, like milk—it has color and taste but no smell—and by the appearance of two descriptions from a liquid that has three, like vinegar. And overwhelming in a liquid that has no description, like used water or rosewater that has lost its smell, is by weight. So if two ratls of used water mix with one ratl of absolute water, wudu is not permissible with it, and in the opposite case, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a direct ruling (matn) specifying the conditions under which a liquid added to water renders it impure (najis) for wudu or ghusl. The Hanafi school distinguishes three scenarios. First, if the added liquid has only two attributes (e.g., milk: color and taste but no smell), then if mixing causes one attribute of the milk to appear in the water, the milk is considered overwhelming and the mixture becomes impure. Second, if the added liquid has three attributes (e.g., vinegar: color, taste, and smell), then two of its attributes must appear in the water for it to be considered overwhelming. Third, if the added liquid has no distinctive attributes (like used water or scentless rosewater), then overwhelming is determined by quantity: if the added liquid weighs more than half the total mixture, it is overwhelming; otherwise not. For example, two ratls of used water mixed with one ratl of absolute water makes the mixture impure for wudu, but the opposite case is pure.

Key Terms
غلبة (ghalabah): Overwhelming – the state where a mixed substance's attributes or weight dominate the water, causing it to become impure.
مطلق (mutlaq): Absolute water – pure water that is not changed by any impurity or mixture, suitable for wudu and ghusl.
مستعمل (musta'mal): Used water – water that has been used for wudu or ghusl and is no longer absolute; it has no attributes.
راتل (ratl): A unit of weight (approx. 0.45 kg in Hanafi context) used to measure liquids.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A liquid with three attributes (e.g., vinegar) is mixed with water. Under what condition does it become overwhelming?
A) When one of its attributes appears in the water
B) When two of its attributes appear in the water
C) When its weight is more than the water
D) When any attribute appears, regardless of number

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets precise thresholds for ghalabah based on the number of attributes of the added liquid, and when no attributes exist, by weight majority.

Defining Najis Water: When Pure Water Becomes Impure
Defines najis water with quantitative condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of najis water and identify the two conditions that render little still water impure.

Core Idea
Little still water becomes impure the moment impurity falls, even without a trace.

All water is originally pure, but contact with impurity can change its status. The Hanafi school pinpoints exactly when water becomes impure: when it is still (non-flowing) and little (less than 10x10 cubits). In this lesson, we break down that definition and understand why even an invisible impurity suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
The passage introduces the fourth water category: najis water. Pick out its two key characteristics from the text.
The word 'raakidan' means still, and 'qaleelan' means little.
2
Define 'little' quantitatively
The text specifies 'less than ten by ten' (cubits). Understand that this is approximately 5.5m x 5.5m.
This is a fixed threshold in the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the ruling
In such water, impurity immediately makes the water najis even if color, taste, or smell remain unchanged. Why does the school adopt this strict view?
Because the water is small, impurity inevitably contacts the whole.
4
Distinguish from large water
The last hadith in the passage implies that large water requires an attribute change. Recognize that the same impurity in a large pool may not render it impure.
Large water is 10x10 cubits or more, or flowing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع ماء نجس وهو الذي حلّت فيه نجاسة وكان راكدا قليلا والقليل مادون عشر في عشر فينجس وان لم يظهر اثرها فيه لا يبولن احدكم في الماء الدائم الذي لا يجرى ثم يغتسل فيه لا يغتسل احدكم في الماء الدائم وهو جنب اذا استيقظ احدكم من نومه فلا يغمس يده في الاناء حتى يغسلها ثلاثا لا يغسل أحدكم في الماء الدائم وهو جنب ان الماء لا ينجسه شيء الا ما غلب على ريحه وطعمه ولونه
And the fourth is najis water, which is water in which an impurity has fallen and it is still and little. And 'little' is less than ten by ten, so it becomes impure even if its effect does not appear. 'Let none of you urinate in still water that does not flow then bathe in it.' 'Let none of you bathe in still water while in a state of major impurity.' 'When one of you wakes from sleep, let him not dip his hand into the vessel until he washes it three times.' 'Let none of you bathe in still water while in a state of major impurity.' 'Nothing makes water impure except that which overpowers its smell, taste, and color.'
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth category of water: najis water. According to the Hanafi school, water becomes impure (najis) when an impurity falls into it and the water is both still (non-flowing) and little. 'Little' is defined as less than a square of ten cubits by ten cubits (approximately 5.5m x 5.5m). In such water, it becomes impure even if no trace of the impurity appears in its color, taste, or smell. The commentary then provides several Prophetic hadith as proofs. The first hadith forbids urinating in still water and then bathing in it, implying that the water becomes impure. The second and fourth hadith forbid a junub person from bathing in still water, again indicating impurity. The third hadith commands washing hands after sleep before dipping into a vessel, showing that contact with impurity (the hand) can render the water impure. The fifth hadith is for large water bodies: it remains pure unless an attribute changes. Thus, the ruling distinguishes between little still water (impure by mere contact) and large or flowing water (impure only if attribute changes).

Key Terms
ماء نجس (ma' najis): Water that is legally impure and cannot be used for purification. It is either due to impurity falling into little still water or any change in large water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two conditions that must be present for water to become najis in the Hanafi school?
A) Still and little
B) Flowing and little
C) Still and large
D) Any water with trace

Key Takeaway
Little still water becomes impure immediately upon contact with impurity, even if no trace appears, due to the Hanafi quantitative rule.

The Prophetic Proofs for Najis Water
Lists hadith proofs for the ruling on najis water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the cited hadith prove that little still water becomes impure and distinguish the evidence for large water.

Core Idea
The Prophet's prohibitions logically imply impurity; otherwise the prohibitions would be meaningless.

The previous lesson established the definition. Now we examine the textual evidence. The passage cites several hadith that, according to the Hanafi school, provide the legal basis for the ruling on najis water. Understanding how each hadith serves as proof is essential for grasping the school's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first hadith
The Prophet forbade urinating in still water and then bathing. Why does this prove impurity?
If the water remained pure, the prohibition would be mere etiquette.
2
Identify the second and fourth hadith
These forbid a junub person from bathing in still water. This shows that contact with janabah renders the water impure.
The prohibition is about the water being used for purification after being soiled by an impure person.
3
Identify the third hadith
The command to wash hands after sleep before dipping into a vessel implies that if you dip without washing, the water becomes impure.
This is an inference (dalil) that the hand is considered potentially impure after sleep.
4
Identify the fifth hadith
This states that water is not made impure except by overpowering its attributes. How does this apply?
This is interpreted as applying to large water, not to the small still water covered by the previous hadith.
5
Synthesize the evidence
Together, the hadith establish a two-tier ruling: small still water is impure by mere contact; large water requires a change in attributes.
The Hanafi usul reconciles the specific prohibitions with the general statement.
